Obligation to Remove Structures After Lease Ends
States that lessee must remove buildings and trees and return land empty.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on removal of lessee's structures after lease expiry and explain the legal basis of harm prevention.

Core Idea
Lessee must remove all fixtures to avoid indefinite harm to the owner.

When a lease ends, the land must be returned empty. But what if the lessee has built a structure or planted trees? This lesson examines the Hanafi ruling that the lessee must remove them, based on the principle that ongoing presence causes harm to the owner.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
What is the lessee required to do? State the hukm level (wajib, sunnah, etc.) and the specific action.
The text says 'قلعهما' (uproot both) and 'تسليمها فارغة' (hand it over empty).
2
Understand the legal reasoning (illa)
Why is removal obligatory? The text gives the reason: 'لأنه لا نهاية لهما ففي إبقائهما إضرار' (because they have no end, so keeping them causes harm). Harm (idrar) is the effective cause.
This follows the maxim 'la darar wa la dirar'.
3
Apply the ruling to concrete cases
Consider different types of fixtures (buildings, trees, removable structures). Does the same ruling apply to all? The key is whether they have a 'natural end' or cause harm if left indefinitely.
Temporary, easily removable items may not cause the same harm.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله: (فإن مضت المدة قلعهما وسلمها فارغة) لأنه لا نهاية لهما ففي إبقائهما إضرار بصاحب الأرض فوجب
His statement: (Then if the term passes, he uproots both and hands it over empty) because there is no end to them, so in keeping them is harm to the owner of the land, so it is compulsory.
Source pages p. 519

Commentary
This is a direct commentary on the matn. If the lessee has planted trees or built structures on the leased land, when the lease term expires, he must remove them and return the land empty (without any of his property). The reason given is that these things have no natural end (they would continue indefinitely), and leaving them would cause harm (dharar) to the landowner, so removal is obligatory.

Key Terms
قلع (qal'): Uprooting or removal of structures and trees from the land.
إضرار (idrar): Causing harm; here it is the basis for obligating removal.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why must the lessee remove buildings and trees after the lease term ends?
A) Because they belong to the owner
B) Because keeping them would harm the owner
C) Because the lease is void
D) By mutual agreement

Key Takeaway
A lessee must remove all structures and trees after lease expiry to return the land empty, as their indefinite presence harms the owner.

Brick-Making Ijarah: The Correct Preposition
Correction of matn wording in ijarah for brick making

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the correct wording for leasing land for brick making is 'from it' (minha) rather than 'in it' (fiha) and identify the legal implication for ijarah rulings.

Core Idea
In brick-making ijarah, the soil is consumed, so the lease is on the substance, not the benefit.

This lesson examines a subtle but critical correction in the matn of Kanz al-Daqa'iq. Al-Ramli, commenting on the phrase 'to make bricks in it,' insists the correct wording is 'to make bricks from it,' citing al-Khaniyyah. The distinction is not pedantic—it reflects a fundamental principle of Hanafi ijarah: when the subject matter is consumed, the contract is a lease of substance rather than benefit, affecting validity and pricing.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Act and the Disputed Phrasing
The act is ijarah (leasing) of land for making bricks (laban). The matn originally said 'ليلبن فيها' (to make bricks in it). Your task is to note the preposition 'فيها' (in it).
The dispute is about the preposition—does it indicate using the space or consuming the soil?
2
Understand the Legal Basis for the Correction
Al-Ramli states the correct form is 'منها' (from it) because 'الإجارة وقعت على العين' (the ijarah falls on the substance). This means the contract covers the soil itself, which is consumed in the brick-making process.
In Hanafi fiqh, ijarah normally covers benefits (manafi'), but when the substance is used up, it becomes an ijarah on the substance (ijarah 'ala al-ayn).
3
Apply the Correction to the Contract
The proper wording must reflect that the lessee acquires the right to extract and consume the soil. Thus, the price should correspond to the value of the soil, not just the use of the land surface.
Incorrect wording ('in it') could make the contract invalid (fasid) because it fails to specify the subject matter accurately.
4
Trace the Source of the Correction
Al-Ramli attributes this correction to al-Khaniyyah, a Hanafi authority. This shows the importance of referencing reliable later sources (fatawa mutakhkhirin) for precise legal formulations.
Always check later commentaries for refinements of earlier matns.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله: (ليلبن فيها) قال الرملي صوابه منها كما في الخانية قائلاً لوقوع الإجارة على العين
His statement: (to make bricks in it) Al-Ramli said: its correct form is 'from it' as in al-Khaniyyah, saying because the ijarah falls on the substance.
Source pages p. 519

Commentary
This is a brief note from al-Ramli correcting the matn's wording. The matn likely said 'to make bricks in it' but al-Ramli says the correct phrase is 'to make bricks from it' (minha) because the ijarah contract applies to the soil itself (the substance) being used up, not just the space. This correction is taken from al-Khaniyyah.

Key Terms
لبن (laban (here labin)): Bricks; the act of making bricks using soil.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to al-Ramli, how should the matn read regarding brick making?
A) To make bricks in it
B) To make bricks from it
C) To make bricks for it
D) To make bricks with it

Key Takeaway
Precision in ijarah wording is essential: 'from it' (minha) signals a lease of substance, while 'in it' (fiha) can render the contract fasid.

The Meaning of 'Sabeel' in Waqf and Ijarah
Defines 'sabeel' and restates the prohibition

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define 'sabeel' as a type of waqf and state the ruling regarding building by its lessee.

Core Idea
Sabeel is a waqf for the general public; its lessee may not build a room for himself.

The term 'sabeel' appears in fiqh rulings related to ijarah and waqf. In this short passage, al-Ramli clarifies its meaning by referring back to the chapter on waqf. Understanding this definition is essential to correctly apply the prohibition on the lessee from building for his own benefit.

Guided Path
1
Identify the term being defined
The text defines 'sabeel'. It is a waqf for the general public (al-waqf 'ala al-'ammah).
2
Apply the definition to the ruling
The ruling prohibits the lessee of a sabeel from building a room for himself. This prohibition arises because the sabeel is for public benefit, not private use.

The Lessee's Right to Retain Structures on Waqf Land: An Intra-Hanafi Specification
Ruling on waqf land lease and whether lessee can be forced to remove trees/buildings

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi position on whether beneficiaries of a waqf can compel the lessee to remove buildings/trees after lease expiry, and identify the basis for the exceptional rule in waqf.

Core Idea
Beneficiaries of a waqf cannot force removal of lessee's buildings/trees—the general rule is specified to preserve both parties' interests.

This lesson examines a specific dispute within the Hanafi school regarding the lessee of waqf land who, with permission, plants or builds. The general rule in Ijarah law requires the lessee to remove all additions and return the land empty. However, for waqf land, leading scholars like Al-Ramli and the author of Awqaf al-Khassaf argue that the lessee may retain the structures even after the lease ends, and the beneficiaries cannot compel removal. We will trace the reasoning and the justification for this specification.

Guided Path
1
State the general rule in Ijarah
In ordinary lease contracts, the lessee must remove all structures and plants after the lease term ends and restore the land to its original condition.
This rule is based on the principle that the lessee's right to use the land is temporary.
2
Identify the contested case
The dispute arises when the leased land is waqf property and the lessee, with the permission of the beneficiaries, has planted trees or built structures.
Permission is a key condition for the exception.
3
Present the opposing views
Some Hanafi scholars (based on the apparent meaning of the matn) insist that the lessee must remove, as the general rule demands. Others, led by Al-Ramli and the work Awqaf al-Khassaf, argue that the lessee may retain the improvements.
This is an intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf.
4
Analyze the justification for the exception
The justification is twofold: (1) it is possible to consider the interests of both the lessee and the beneficiaries without causing harm; (2) forced removal would serve no benefit because the land would not fetch a higher rent without the structures.
This reasoning is based on the principle of avoiding harm (la darar wa la dirar).
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical outcome is that the lessee (and his heirs) may keep the buildings/trees after the lease ends, and the beneficiaries cannot demand their removal. This specification protects the lessee's investment and preserves the utility of the waqf.
This ruling applies only when the lessee had permission to build/plant.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقوله: (وفي القنية استأجر أرضاً وقفاً وغرس فيها أو بنى الخ) قال الرملي : ذكرها بعد أن رمز (سم) (قع) لإسماعيل المتكلم أو هو بالمعجمة لشرف الأئمة المكي والقاضي عبد الجبار وقال فيها : قيل لهما فلو أبي الموقوف عليهم إلا القلع هل لهم ذلك؟ قال : لا وقد قالوا لا تعويل ولا التفات إلى كل ما قاله صاحب القنية مخالفاً للقواعد ما لم يعضده نقل من غيره وقد عضد بما في أوقاف الخصاف ووجهه إمكان رعاية الجانبين من غير ضرر فعليه إذا مات أحدهما فللمستأجر أو ورثته الاستبقاء فيكون مخصصاً لكلام المتون. ووجهه أيضاً عدم الفائدة في القلع إذ لو قلع لا تؤجر بأكثر
And his statement: (And in al-Qunyah: He leased waqf land and planted or built in it) Al-Ramli said: He mentioned it after he encoded 'sm' and 'q' for Isma'il al-Mutakallim or it is with a dotted letter for the honor of the Imams al-Makki and al-Qadi 'Abd al-Jabbar. And he said in it: It was said to them: if the beneficiaries (mawquf 'alayhim) refuse except uprooting, do they have that? He said: No. And they said: No reliance nor attention to everything that the author of al-Qunyah said contrary to the rules unless it is supported by transmission from others. And it is supported by what is in Awqaf al-Khassaf, and its justification is the possibility of considering both sides without harm. Therefore, when one of them dies, the lessee or his heirs may keep [the structures], thus it is a specification of the statements of the matns. And its justification is also the lack of benefit in uprooting, because if uprooted, it will not be leased for more.
Source pages p. 519

Commentary
This is a commentary by al-Ramli on a ruling from al-Qunyah regarding a lessee who planted trees or built on waqf land. The issue is whether the beneficiaries (mawquf 'alayhim) can force the lessee to remove them after the lease ends. Al-Ramli refers to earlier scholars (Isma'il al-Mutakallim, Imams al-Makki, and al-Qadi 'Abd al-Jabbar) who said they cannot. He then states that statements from al-Qunyah that contradict established rules are not relied upon unless supported by other evidence. Here, it is supported by a text from Awqaf al-Khassaf, which argues that both sides (the lessee's right to use and the beneficiaries' right) can be preserved without harm. Therefore, if one party dies, the lessee or his heirs may retain the structures. This is a specification of the general ruling in the matns that the lessee must remove them. The justification is that forced removal would be useless because the land would not fetch a higher rent afterward.

Key Terms
موقوف عليهم (mawquf 'alayhim): The beneficiaries of a waqf, who have the right to benefit from the property.
قلع (qal'): Uprooting or removal of structures/trees.
استبقاء (istibqa'): Retention; the right of the lessee to keep the structures after lease expiry.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Yes, always
B) No, they cannot
C) Only if the trees are fully grown
D) Only if the lease was for one year

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, a lessee of waqf land who planted or built with permission may retain the structures after lease ends; beneficiaries cannot force removal.

Ruling on Buildings and Trees on Leased Land After Lease Expiry
Explains the rule for buildings and trees on leased land after lease ends.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on buildings/trees on leased land after lease expiry, identify the condition for forced ownership, and apply the rule to a scenario.

Core Idea
After lease expiry, the lessor may pay value of building and keep it—but forced only if removal damages land.

When a lease ends, the lessee's building or trees on the land must generally be removed. However, Hanafi fiqh provides the lessor an option to pay the value of the structure and keep it in place, preventing waste. This lesson unpacks the rule, the condition for forced ownership (jabr), and how the ruling applies to different situations, including waqf property.

Guided Path
1
Identify the default obligation of the lessee
Upon lease expiry, the lessee is obligated (wajib) to remove any buildings or trees he erected on the land. This is the default rule to restore the land to the lessor.
This obligation is contractual, not devotional (fard).
2
Understand the lessor's option to pay and keep
The lessor has the option to pay the lessee the value of the building/tree as if uprooted (qal') and take ownership of it. If the lessor chooses this option, the lessee is no longer obligated to remove.
The value is appraised as the structure when uprooted (mumtani' al-qal'), not cost of construction.
3
Distinguish forced ownership (jabr) from consensual
The crucial nuance: the lessor can only force (jabr) the lessee to accept payment and surrender the building if removing the structure would damage the land. If no damage, the lessee's consent is required.
This condition prevents unnecessary harm to the land.
4
Apply to different scenarios
If removal damages the land, the lessor can compel ownership. If no damage, he must negotiate. Alternatively, the lessor may allow the building to remain, which constitutes a loan ('ariyah) if free, or a new lease if rent is charged.
The lessor and lessee may also jointly lease the building to a third party and split the rent proportionally.
5
Note the waqf application
In waqf property, if a lessee builds without permission and removal harms the waqf, the custodian is compelled to pay compensation and keep the building. This mirrors the jabr principle.
Permission for planting trees may be given if beneficial and not harmful.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كالشجر والزرع يترك بأجر المثل إلى أن يدرك والدابة للركوب والحمل والثوب للبس ذلك اهـ. وبهذا يعلم مسألة الأرض المحتكرة وهي منقولة أيضاً في أوقاف الخصاف قوله : (إلا أن يغرم له المؤجر قيمته مقلوعاً ويتملكه يعني بأن تقوم الأرض بدون البناء والشجر ويقوم بها بناء أو شجر لصاحب الأرض أن يأمره بقلعه فيضمن فضل ما بينهما؛ كذا في الاختيار. وهذا الاستثناء راجع إلى لزوم القلع على المستأجر فأفاد أنه إذا رضي المؤجر يدفع القيمة لا يلزم المستأجر القلع وهذا صحيح مطلقاً، سواء كانت الأرض تنقص بالقلع أو لا، فلا حاجة إلى حمل كلام المصنف على ما إذا كانت الأرض تنقص بالقلع كما فعل الشارح تبعاً لغيره لكن لا يتملكها المؤجر جبراً على المستأجر إلا إذا كانت الأرض تنقص بالقلع، وأما إذا كانت لا تنقص فلا بد من رضاه قوله : (أو) يرضى بتركه فيكون البناء والغرس لهذا والأرض لهذا) يعني إذا رضي المؤجر بترك البناء أو الغرس لا يلزم المستأجر القلع فلا حاجة إلى جعل الضمير في يرضى عائداً إلى كل منهما ولا إلى التصريح برضاهما كما وقع في المجمع كما لا يخفى. وهذا الترك من المؤجر يكون عارية لأرضه إن كان بغير أجر وإجارة، وإن كان بأجر فقصره في غاية البيان على الأول مما لا ينبغي، وعلى الأول لهما أن يؤجراهما من أجنبي فإن فعلا فلهما أن يقسما الأجر على قيمة الأرض من غير بناء، وعلى قيمة البناء من غير أرض فيأخذ كل واحد منهما حصته؛ كذا في شرح الأقطع وفي القنية من الوقف: بنى في الدار المسيلة بغير إذن القيم ونزع البناء يضر بالوقف يجبر القيم على دفع قيمته للباني، ويجوز للمستأجرين غرس الأشجار والكروم في الموقوفة إذا لم يضر بالأرض بدون صريح الإذن من المتولي دون حفر الحياض. وإنما يحل للمتولي الإذن فيما يزيد الوقف به خيراً وهذا إذا لم يكن لهم حق قرار العمارة فيها، أما إذا كان يجوز الحفر والغرس والحائط من ترابها لوجود الإذن في مثلها دلالة اهـ
Such as trees and crops are left with equivalent rent until they ripen, and the animal for riding and carrying, and the garment for wearing, that is it. And by this is known the issue of the monopolized land (al-ard al-muhtakara), and it is also transmitted in Awqaf al-Khassaf. His statement: (Except that the lessor pays him its value uprooted and takes ownership of it) meaning that the land is valued without the building and trees, and the building or trees are valued; the landowner has the right to order him to uproot it, and he guarantees the difference between them; thus in al-Ikhtiyar. And this exception refers to the binding nature of uprooting on the lessee, so it indicates that if the lessor agrees to pay the value, the lessee is not obligated to uproot, and this is absolutely correct, whether the land is diminished by uprooting or not, so there is no need to interpret the author's statement as being when the land is diminished by uprooting as the commentator did following others. But the lessor does not take ownership of it by compulsion over the lessee unless the land is diminished by uprooting; but if it is not diminished, then his consent is necessary. His statement: (or) he agrees to leave it, so the building and planting belong to this one and the land to this one) meaning if the lessor agrees to leave the building or planting, the lessee is not obligated to uproot, so there is no need to make the pronoun in 'he agrees' refer to both of them, nor to explicitly mention both of their agreement as occurred in al-Majma', as is not hidden. And this leaving by the lessor is a loan for his land if it is without rent, and a lease if it is with rent; limiting it to the first is not appropriate. And according to the first, both of them may lease them to a third party; if they do so, they divide the rent according to the value of the land without the building and the value of the building without the land, so each takes his share; thus in Sharh al-Aqta'. And in al-Quniya concerning waqf: one built in the mosque without the permission of the custodian, and removing the building harms the waqf, the custodian is compelled to pay its value to the builder. And it is permissible for lessees to plant trees and vines in the waqf property if it does not harm the land, without explicit permission from the trustee, but not for digging cisterns. And the trustee may only give permission for what increases the waqf's benefit, and this is if they do not have the right to settle the buildings there; but if they do, then digging, planting, and building walls from its soil are permitted by implicit permission.
Source pages p. 521

Commentary
This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq is a commentary on the ruling about buildings and trees on leased land after the lease expires. The author begins by mentioning the general rule that trees and crops are left with an equivalent rent (ajr al-mithl) until they ripen, but then provides an exception: the lessor may pay the value of the building or tree when uprooted and take ownership of it. The commentary then explains that this exception modifies the obligation of the lessee to remove the structure. If the lessor agrees to pay the value, the lessee is not forced to uproot. However, there is a dispute within the Hanafi school: some hold that this is absolute, while others, including the commentator, restrict it to cases where uprooting would damage the land. The relied-upon position (mufta bihi) is that the lessor can only force ownership of the building/plant if removal would damage the land; otherwise, the lessee's consent is required. The passage also discusses the scenario where the lessor allows the building/plant to remain; this amounts to a loan ('ariyah) if without rent, or a new lease if with rent. If both parties agree, they may also lease the building/plant to a third party and divide the rent proportionally. The text further cites a ruling from al-Quniya about waqf land: if a building is constructed without permission and its removal harms the waqf, the custodian is compelled to pay compensation to the builder. The overall lesson emphasizes the Hanafi balancing of rights: the lessor's right to reclaim the land, the lessee's investment in improvements, and the importance of preventing damage.

Key Terms
قلع (qal'): Uprooting or removal of the building or tree from the land.
غرم (ghurm): To pay compensation; here the lessor pays the value of the building/plant to the tenant.
جبر (jabr): Compulsion; the lessor may compel the tenant to accept payment and give up the building/plant only if removal damages the land.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In Hanafi fiqh, when can the lessor forcibly take ownership of a building built by the tenant on leased land after the lease ends?
A) In all cases, because the landlord owns the land.
B) Only if removing the building would damage the land.
C) Only if the tenant agrees.
D) Only if the building is of no value.

Key Takeaway
The lessor's right to forcibly own the tenant's building is conditional on preventing land damage.

Ruling on Fresh Greens and Crops on Leased Land After Lease Expiry
Distinguishes between fresh greens and crops on leased land after lease expiry.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between fresh greens (rutba) and crops (zar') on leased land after lease expiry, and apply the correct ruling: removal or retention with equivalent rent.

Core Idea
Fresh greens with no fixed end must be removed; crops with a known harvest are left with equivalent rent.

When a lease ends but the land still contains plants, the Hanafi school distinguishes based on the nature of the plant. This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq, a commentary on Kanz al-Daqa'iq, explains the ruling and its rational basis. You will learn the criteria that determine whether the tenant must uproot the plants or may leave them until harvest, and how this ruling applies to borrowers and usurpers.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two types of vegetation
The passage distinguishes based on whether the plant has a fixed end (nihayah) or not. First, note whether the plant is rutba (fresh greens like alfalfa) or zar' (crops like wheat).
2
Apply the analogy for rutba
Since rutba has no fixed end, it is analogous to trees and must be uprooted. The illa is the absence of a known harvest time.
This is a qiyas jali (clear analogy).
3
Apply the rule for zar'
Because zar' has a known end, it is left with ajr al-mithl (equivalent rent) until harvest. This balances the rights of the lessor and lessee.
The rent is not automatic; it requires a court order or mutual agreement.
4
Handle exceptional cases
Consider modifications: if one party dies before ripening, the crop is left with the stipulated rent (musamma) until the end of the original term. The borrower (musta'ir) is treated like a lessee; the usurper (ghasib) must uproot absolutely.
Death does not change the treatment; the crop stays with the original rent. The usurper's initial injustice changes the ruling.
5
Memorize the concluding point
The rent for the crop is only due by a judge's decision or their agreement, not automatically. This is a key procedural detail.
This ensures the rent is not imposed without authority.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والرطبة كالشجر ولهذا قال في الجامع الصغير: وإذا انقضت مدة الإجارة وفي الأرض رطبة فإنها تقلع لأن الرطاب لا نهاية لها فأشبه الشجر. قوله: (والزرع يترك بأجر المثل إلى أن يدرك) لأن له نهاية معلومة فأمكن رعاية الجانبين إذا انقضت مدة الإجارة بخلاف موت أحدهما قبل إدراكه فإنه يترك بالمسمى على حالة إلى الحصاد وإن انفسخت الإجارة لأن إبقاءه على ما كان أولى ما دامت المدة باقية، ويلحق بالمستأجر المستعير فيترك إلى إدراكه بأجرة المثل وخرج الغاصب فإنه يؤمر بالقلع مطلقاً لأن ابتداء الفعل ظلم وهو واجب الهدم لا التقرير. وفي التقرير المراد بقول الفقهاء إذا انتهت الإجارة والزرع لم يستحصد يترك بأجر أي بقضاء أو بعقدهما حتى لا يجب الأجر إلا بأحدهما اهـ. وهو مما يجب حفظه
And fresh greens (al-rutba) are like trees, and for this reason it is said in al-Jami' al-Saghir: When the term of the lease expires and there is fresh greens in the land, they are uprooted because fresh greens have no end, so they resemble trees. His statement: (And the crop is left with equivalent rent until it is ready to harvest) because it has a known end, so it is possible to consider both sides when the lease term expires. Unlike the death of one of them before its ripening, then it is left with the stipulated rent (al-musamma) in its state until the harvest, even if the lease is rescinded, because keeping it as it was is preferable as long as the term remains. And the borrower (al-musta'ir) is treated like the lessee, so it is left until its ripening with equivalent rent. And the usurper (al-ghasib) is excluded, for he is ordered to uproot absolutely, because the initial act was injustice, and he is obligated to demolish, not to confirm. And in the report (al-taqrir), what is meant by the jurists' statement: When the lease ends and the crop has not ripened, it is left with rent, meaning by a judge's decision or by mutual agreement, so that the rent is not obligatory except by one of them. It is something that must be memorized.
Source pages p. 522

Commentary
This passage differentiates between two types of vegetation on leased land after the lease expires. The first type is الرطبة (al-rutba), which refers to fresh greens like alfalfa or clover that regrow repeatedly. Because such plants have no fixed end—they can be harvested multiple times indefinitely—they are treated like trees: they must be uprooted when the lease ends. The second type is الزرع (al-zar'), which refers to crops like wheat or barley that have a fixed harvest time. Since their harvest is determined, it is possible to accommodate both the lessor (who wants his land back) and the lessee (who wants to harvest the crop). Thus, the crop is left in the ground with an equivalent rent (ajr al-mithl) until it ripens. The passage also notes a special case: if one of the parties (lessor or lessee) dies before the crop ripens, the crop is left with the stipulated rent (al-musamma) until harvest, because the lease remains in effect until the end of the term. Even if the lease is rescinded (due to death), the crop is still left with the same rent. The ruling extends to the borrower (musta'ir) under a loan contract (i'arah) as well: the crop is left with equivalent rent. However, the usurper (ghasib) is treated differently: he is ordered to uproot the crop immediately, because his initial possession was unlawful. The passage emphasizes that the jurists' rule that the crop is left with rent means that the rent is only due if there is a court order or mutual agreement, not automatically. This is a key point to remember.

Key Terms
رطبة (rutba): Fresh greens, such as alfalfa, that regrow after cutting and have no fixed harvest season.
أجر المثل (ajr al-mithl): The fair market rent for the land or property for the period the crop occupies it.
غاصب (ghasib): A usurper who took the land without any legal right; his possession is unlawful.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why are fresh greens (al-rutba) treated like trees in Hanafi law when the lease expires?
A) Because they are perennial plants.
B) Because they have no fixed end or harvest time.
C) Because they damage the land.
D) Because they are not considered crops.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school distinguishes between fresh greens (must uproot) and crops (leave with rent) based on whether the plant has a fixed end, reflecting the balance between the lessor's right to land and the lessee's investment.

Valid and Invalid Uses in Ijarah: The Requirement of Customary Intended Benefit
Ruling on valid and invalid uses in ijarah with examples

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify whether a particular use of a leased object is valid (sahih) or invalid (fasid) according to the Hanafi school, applying the criterion of customary intended benefit (manfa'ah maqsudah).

Core Idea
Leasing for display or non-customary use is invalid; the benefit must be the object's intended purpose.

This lesson examines the Hanafi position on permissible uses of leased objects. The key principle is that the leased object must provide a known, customary, and intended benefit. Animals are leased for riding and carrying, garments for wearing; leasing for pure display or prestige is invalid. We will explore examples and understand the rationale behind these rulings.

Guided Path
1
State the general rule for valid ijarah
Recognize that a valid lease requires the benefit to be intended (maqsudah), customary (ma'hudah), and known (ma'lumah). This is the foundation for evaluating specific uses.
2
Identify invalid uses for animals
Read the examples: leasing an animal to lead it without riding, or to tie it for show. Conclude that these are invalid because the benefit is not riding or carrying. Note the ruling: ijarah fasidah, no rent due.
3
Identify invalid uses for garments and other objects
Similarly, leasing a garment for decoration, a vessel for display, a house not to inhabit, or a slave not to use – all are invalid. Extend the principle to any object used for non-customary purposes.
4
Apply the rule to new scenarios
Practice with examples: leasing a car for a road trip (valid) vs. leasing a car to park it in the driveway for show (invalid). Use the criterion of customary intended benefit.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والدابة للركوب والحمل والثوب للبس أي صح استئجار الدابة والثوب لأن المنفعة مقصودة معهودة معلومة. قيد بالركوب والحمل لأنه لو استأجر دابة ليجنبها ولا يركبها أو ليربطها على باب داره ليرى الناس أن له فرساً فالإجارة فاسدة ولا أجر له. وقيد باللبس في الثوب لأنه لو استأجر ثوباً ليزين بيته به أو حانوته فالإجارة فاسدة، ومن هذا النوع ما إذا استأجر آنية يصفها في بيته يتحمل بها ولا يستعملها أو داراً لا يسكنها لكن ليظن الناس أن له داراً أو عبداً على أن لا يستخدمه أو دراهم يضعها؛ كذا في أن هذه المنفعة ليست مقصودة من العين كما قدمناه أول الكتاب، وخرج أيضاً ما إذا استأجر فحلاً لينزيه على أنثى فإنه لا يجوز. وفي الخلاصة: معاوضة الثيران في الكراب لا خير فيها، أما إذا أعطى البقر ليأخذ الحمار جاز ويكفي في استئجار الثوب للبس التمكن منه وإن لم يلبس لما في الخلاصة: رجل استأجر ثوباً ليلبسه كل يوم بدانق فوضعه في بيته سنين ولم يلبسه رد لكل يوم دانق إلى الوقت الذي لو لبسه إلى ذلك الوقت لتخرق فحينئذ سقط الأجر بعد ذلك اهـ. وهو كالسكنى قال في المجمع ويجب بنفس القبض وإن لم يسكنها، وفي الدابة لا يكفي التمكن لما في فصول العمادي من الفصل الثاني والثلاثين: ولو استأجر دابة ليركبها إلى مكان معلوم فأمسكها في منزله في المصر لا يحب الأجر ويضمن لو
And the animal is for riding and carrying, and the garment is for wearing, meaning that leasing the animal and garment is valid because the benefit is intended, customary, and known. It is restricted to riding and carrying because if he leases an animal to lead it without riding it, or to tie it at the door of his house so that people see that he has a horse, the lease is invalid (fasid) and he has no rent. And it is restricted to wearing for the garment because if he leases a garment to decorate his house or shop with it, the lease is invalid. And of this type is if he leases a vessel to display in his house for show without using it, or a house not to live in but so that people think he has a house, or a slave on condition that he not use him, or dirhams to put them; thus these benefits are not intended from the object as we presented at the beginning of the book. And also excluded is if he leases a stallion to mate it with a female, for it is not permissible. And in al-Khulasa: transacting bulls for plowing has no good in it; but if he gives the cow to take the donkey, it is permissible. And it is sufficient for leasing a garment for wearing that he is able to wear it even if he does not wear it, because in al-Khulasa: a man leased a garment to wear every day for a daniq, and he placed it in his house for years and did not wear it; he must pay a daniq for each day up to the time when if he had worn it up to that time it would have worn out; then after that the rent drops. It is like dwelling: it is said in al-Majma': rent is due upon taking possession even if he does not dwell. But for an animal, mere ability is not sufficient, because in Fusul al-'Imadi, chapter thirty-two: If he leases an animal to ride to a known place and then holds it in his house in the city, he is not liable for the rent, but he is liable if...
Source pages p. 522

Commentary
This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq explains the valid uses of animals and garments in ijarah (lease) contracts. The general principle is that the leased object must provide a known, customary, and intended benefit. For animals, the typical benefits are riding and carrying loads; if the animal is leased for pure display (e.g., to be seen as a possession) without actual use, the contract is invalid (fasid) and no rent is due. Similarly, garments are leased for wearing; using them as decoration in a house or shop invalidates the lease. The text extends this to other objects: vessels displayed for show, houses not intended for dwelling, slaves not used, and dirhams for display—all are invalid because the benefit is not the intended use of the object. The passage also prohibits leasing a stallion for mating (because it involves a benefit that is not customary property?). Then it discusses the issue of payment when the lessee is able to use but does not. For garments, if the tenant has the ability to wear the garment (even if he doesn't), he must pay rent until the garment would have worn out from normal use; after that, no more rent. This is analogous to dwelling: in a house lease, rent is due upon taking possession even if he doesn't live there. However, for an animal, mere ability is not enough; if the tenant holds the animal without using it, he is not liable for rent (but is liable for damages if the animal is destroyed). This distinction is based on the nature of the benefit.

Key Terms
إجارة فاسدة (ijarah fasidah): An invalid lease; here it means the contract does not produce the usual legal effects (no obligation to pay rent, but the object must be returned).
منفعة مقصودة (manfa'ah maqsudah): An intended benefit that is customary and known; the object is typically used for this purpose.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which of the following leases is valid according to the Hanafi school?
A) Leasing a horse to tie it at the door for show.
B) Leasing a garment to decorate a shop.
C) Leasing a donkey for carrying loads.
D) Leasing a house to display it but not live in.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi ijarah, the benefit must be the object's customary intended use; otherwise the contract is invalid (fasid) and no rent is due.

The Timing of Rent: When Ability to Use (Tamakkun) Triggers Payment
Distinction in rent liability between garments/houses and animals

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rule that for garments and houses, rent is due upon possession (tamakkun), but for animals, mere ability to use does not trigger rent.

Core Idea
Ability to use (tamakkun) triggers rent for garments/houses but not for animals.

This lesson focuses on a specific nuance in ijarah: when must the lessee pay rent if he has the ability to use the object but does not? According to the Hanafi school, there is a distinction between 'still objects' like garments and houses, and 'mobile objects' like animals. Understanding this distinction is crucial for determining rent obligations.

Guided Path
1
Recall the general rule from the text
The passage states that for a garment, it is sufficient that the tenant is able to wear it (tamakkun) even if he does not wear it. Rent is due for each day until the garment would have worn out from normal use.
2
Apply the analogy to houses
The text compares to dwelling: according to al-Majma', rent is due upon taking possession (qabd) even if the tenant does not dwell. So for houses, tamakkun triggers rent.
3
Contrast with animals
For animals, mere tamakkun is not enough. The text quotes Fusul al-'Imadi: if a man leases an animal to ride to a place and holds it in the city, he is not liable for rent (but is liable for damages).
The text is truncated, but the distinction is clear.
4
Identify the rationale
The rationale is that the benefit of garments and houses is actual possession and availability, while the benefit of animals is actual use (riding/loading). An animal can be held without being used, so rent is not due until use.
5
Apply to modern analogues
Consider leasing a car (mobile) vs. a parking spot (immobile). For a car, rent is due when you drive it? But the rule might be different; apply the principle: the benefit of a car is driving, so possession alone may not trigger rent. However, for a parking spot, possession triggers rent.
This is an extension; the classical text only covers animals and garments/houses.

Key Terms
تمكن (tamakkun): Ability or power to use the leased item. For garments and houses, ability triggers rent; for animals, it does not.

Moist Plants and Crops After Ijarah Ends
Ruling with proof from al-Jami' al-Saghir

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between Hanafi rulings for moist plants (uprooted) and crops (left with rent equivalent) when a lease ends, and apply the correct consequence based on the plant's nature.

Core Idea
Moist plants (ruttab) are uprooted; crops (zar') are left with rent — the difference is knowable termination.

In ijarah, when the lease period expires or terminates, the land may contain growing vegetation. The Hanafi school makes a critical distinction based on whether the plant has a fixed lifespan. This lesson unpacks the ruling from al-Jami' al-Saghir and its underlying reasoning (ta'lil), including exceptions for death of a party and usurpation.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two categories
From the passage, distinguish between moist plants (ruttab) like green fodder with no fixed lifespan, and crops (zar') like wheat with a known maturation period.
The term 'ruttab' refers to any plant that does not have a defined time of harvest; 'zar'' is crops that ripen at a known time.
2
State the ruling for each
Ruttab: must be uprooted immediately when lease ends. Zar': left with rent equivalent (ajr al-mithl) until they ripen, then harvested.
The ruling is from al-Jami' al-Saghir and is mufta bihi in the Hanafi school.
3
Understand the reasoning (ta'lil)
Ruttab has no limit so it resembles trees, which are permanent. Crops have a known end, so it is possible to consider both parties' interests by allowing them to stay with compensation.
The passage explicitly gives the reason: 'because moist plants have no limit' and crops 'have a known end'.
4
Note exceptions and special cases
If one party dies before crops ripen, the crop is left with the named rent (not equivalent) until harvest. A borrower (musta'ir) is treated like a lessee. A usurper (ghasib) is ordered to uproot even crops, because his initial act was wrongful.
The death exception keeps the contract rent; the usurper exception emphasizes that wrongdoing does not receive leniency.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فيأخذ كل واحد منهما حصته ؛ كذا في شرح الأقطع وفي القنية من الوقف : بنى في الدار المسيلة بغير إذن القيم ونزع البناء يضر بالوقف يجبر القيم على دفع قيمته للباني، ويجوز للمستأجرين غرس الأشجار والكروم في الموقوفة إذا لم يضر بالأرض بدون صريح الإذن من المتولي دون حفر الحياض. وإنما يحل للمتولي الإذن فيما يزيد الوقف به خيراً وهذا إذا لم يكن لهم حق قرار العمارة فيها، أما إذا كان يجوز الحفر والغرس والحائط من ترابها لوجود الإذن في مثلها دلالة اهـ قوله : (والرطبة كالشجر ) ولهذا قال في الجامع الصغير : وإذا انقضت مدة الإجارة وفي الأرض رطبة فإنها تقلع لأن الرطاب لا نهاية لها فأشبه الشجر . قوله : (والزرع يترك بأجر المثل إلى أن يدرك لأن له نهاية معلومة فأمكن رعاية الجانبين إذا انقضت مدة الإجارة بخلاف موت أحدهما قبل إدراكه فإنه يترك بالمسمى على حالة إلى الحصاد وإن انفسخت الإجارة لأن إبقاءه على ما كان أولى ما دامت المدة باقية، ويلحق بالمستأجر المستعير فيترك إلى إدراكه بأجرة المثل وخرج الغاصب فإنه يؤمر بالقلع مطلقاً لأن ابتداء الفعل ظلم وهو واجب الهدم لا التقرير. وفي التقرير المراد بقول الفقهاء إذا انتهت الإجارة والزرع لم يستحصد يترك بأجر أي بقضاء أو بعقدهما حتى لا يجب الأجر إلا بأحدهما اهـ. وهو مما يجب حفظه
Each of them takes his share; thus in Sharh al-Aqta' and in al-Qunyah from the section on waqf: If one builds in the mosque (?) dwelling without permission of the administrator and removing the building harms the waqf, the administrator is compelled to pay its value to the builder. It is permissible for tenants to plant trees and vines in the waqf (land) if it does not harm the land, without explicit permission from the trustee, except digging cisterns. The trustee may only permit that which increases the benefit of the waqf, and this is if they have no right of permanent construction. If they do have such right, digging, planting, and wall-building from its soil is permitted due to implicit permission. End. His saying: (Moist plants are like trees). For this reason it is said in al-Jami' al-Saghir: When the lease period ends and there are moist plants in the land, they are uprooted because moist plants have no limit (end) and thus are similar to trees. His saying: (Crops are left with rent equivalent until they ripen) because they have a known end, so it is possible to consider both sides when the lease period ends, unlike the death of one of them before ripening: in that case they are left with the named (agreed) rent until harvest, even if the lease is rescinded, because keeping them as they were is preferable as long as the period remains. The borrower is treated like the lessee: it is left until ripening with rent equivalent. The usurper is excluded: he is ordered to uproot absolutely, because the initial act is oppression and demolition is obligatory, not confirmation. In the explanation, what the jurists mean by 'when the lease ends and the crop has not ripened, it is left with rent' is that it is by decree or by agreement of both parties, so that rent is not owed except by one of them. End. This is something that must be memorized.
Source pages p. 522

Commentary
This passage combines rulings from various sources (Sharh al-Aqta', al-Qunyah) on waqf and ijarah. It begins with a scenario: building without permission in a waqf property where demolition would harm the waqf; the administrator must pay the builder the value. Then it addresses tenants planting trees and vines in waqf land: permissible if no harm, but digging cisterns requires explicit permission. The trustee's permission is only for what increases benefit. The core ruling: after lease ends, moist plants (ruttab) are like trees—they must be uprooted because they have no fixed lifespan (unlike crops). Crops (zar') with a known ripening time are left with rent equivalent until harvest, allowing both parties' interests. If one party dies before ripening, the crop is left with the named rent (not equivalent) until harvest, because keeping status quo is better while the lease period would have continued. The borrower (musta'ir) is treated like the lessee: crop left with rent equivalent. But the usurper (ghasib) is ordered to uproot immediately because his action was wrongful. The final note clarifies that 'left with rent' means it is by judicial decree or mutual agreement, so only one party becomes liable for rent. This is considered an important principle to memorize.

Key Terms
الرطبة (al-ruttab): Moist plants or green fodder that are freshly grown and have no definite lifespan.
الزرع (al-zar'): Crops (like wheat, barley) that have a fixed ripening time.
أجر المثل (ajr al-mithl): Rent equivalent to the prevailing market rate for similar use.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
When a lease ends and the land contains moist plants (ruttab), what is the ruling according to al-Jami' al-Saghir?
A) They are left with rent equivalent until they ripen.
B) They are uprooted because they have no known end.
C) They may be left at the option of the lessee.
D) They become the property of the landowner automatically.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school distinguishes plants by whether they have a known end: unlimited ones (ruttab) are uprooted; limited ones (zar') are left with rent equivalent to avoid harm.

Ijarah — Hire and Lease

Hiring Animals and Garments: Conditions and User Specifications
Details conditions for valid ijarah of animals and garments

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish valid from invalid purposes in ijarah of animals and garments, and apply the Hanafi rules on user specification and liability for deviation.

Core Idea
Ijarah requires a customary, known benefit; specifying the user binds if the use varies, but custom can override strict terms.

This passage from Al-Bahr al-Raiq details the conditions for a valid ijarah contract for animals and garments. It explains which purposes are acceptable (riding, carrying, wearing) and which are not (display, breeding, non-use), and then discusses the consequences of specifying or leaving open the user, including a notable intra-Hanafi dispute on riding an animal hired for carrying. Understanding these rules is essential for correctly structuring ijarah contracts in practice.

Guided Path
1
Identify acceptable purposes
The text states that hiring for riding/carrying and wearing is valid; hiring for display, breeding, or non-use is invalid. Determine if the intended benefit is customary and known.
2
Understand the basis for validity
The benefit must be intended (maqsuda), customary (ma'huda), and known (ma'luma). If these conditions are met, the ijarah is sahih; if not, it is fasid and no rent is due.
3
Distinguish between general and specific user
If the contract gives absolute freedom ('ride whomever you wish'), any rider/wearer is allowed. If a specific user is named, violating that specification may cause liability if the use varies with the user.
4
Analyze the intra-Hanafi dispute
Abu Bakr al-Razi holds that riding when hired for carrying makes the hirer a usurper (liable). Abu al-Layth uses istihsan: custom permits riding, so no liability. The mufta bihi is Abu al-Layth's view.
5
Apply the rule to non-varying uses
If the use does not vary with the user (e.g., dwelling), the restriction is void; the hirer may let others use it without liability.

His saying: (The riding animal for riding and carrying, and the garment for wearing) means that hiring a riding animal and a garment is valid because the benefit is intended, customary, and known. He restricted to riding and carrying because if one hires an animal to keep it company (?) and not ride it, or to tie it at his door so people see he has a horse, the ijarah is invalid and no rent. He restricted to wearing for the garment because if one hires a garment to decorate his house or shop, the ijarah is invalid. From this type is when one hires vessels to display in his house, bearing them and not using them, or a house not to live in but so people think he has a house, or a slave on condition not to use him, or dirhams to place; thus these benefits are not intended from the thing as we mentioned earlier in the book. Also excluded is hiring a stallion to cover a female, which is not permissible. In al-Khulasah: Bartering bulls for plowing is not good; but if he gives a cow to take a donkey, it is permissible. In hiring a garment for wearing, it is sufficient to have the ability to wear it even if one does not wear it, as in al-Khulasah: A man hired a garment to wear each day for a daniq, but he put it in his house for years without wearing it; he must pay a daniq for each day up to the time when, had he worn it until that time, it would have become torn; after that the rent falls. End. This is like dwelling. It is said in al-Majma': It is obligatory by mere possession even if he does not dwell. And for the animal, ability to ride is not sufficient, as in Fusul al-Umadi from the thirty-second chapter: If one hires a riding animal to ride to a known place and keeps it in his house in the city, rent is not obligatory, and he is liable if [it perishes]? The commentators restrict: 'This is if the land is diminished by uprooting.' In his saying 'there is no need to interpret the author's words as being when the land is diminished, there is room for consideration, so reflect.' And if he gives absolute freedom, he may ride and wear whomever he wishes; if he restricts to a specific rider or wearer and then violates, he is liable (if the animal perishes). The same applies to things that vary with the user. But for things that do not vary, the restriction is void, as if he stipulates one dweller, he may let others dwell; if it perishes, [no liability?]. End. In al-Khulasah: If one detains the animal overnight until morning, then returns it without riding, no rent is due. In it also: A man hires an animal to carry loads: he may ride it. If he hires it to ride, he may not carry loads; if he carries loads, no rent is due because riding is called carrying: 'He rode and carried with him another' is said, but carrying is not called riding at all. End. In Fusul al-Umadi citing al-Dhakhirah: He hired an animal to carry wheat from a place to his home for a day until night, and he carried wheat to his home and every time he returned he rode it, and the animal perished. Abu Bakr al-Razi said: He is liable because he hired it for carrying, not riding, so he was a usurper regarding riding. Abu al-Layth said in istihsan: He is not liable because custom among people permits that, so it becomes implicitly permitted even if not explicitly. So the conclusion: They agreed that one who hires for carrying may ride it, but al-Razi conditioned that he not combine both, while al-Layth made it general. His saying: (And if he gives absolute freedom, ride and wear whomever he wishes) – he means by 'absolute' general, i.e., he uses a word indicating generality without restricting to a specific rider or wearer, not the technical 'absolute' of usuliyyun. So if he says 'on condition that you ride whomever you wish' or 'wear whomever you wish', the contract is valid. If he hires it for riding without naming who rides, the ijarah is not valid. The difference is that in the second case the riding is from two persons like two species, making the subject matter unknown; in the first case the owner consented to the benefit occurring within riding, so the subject matter is known. If it is invalid and he lets someone ride or rides himself, the named rent is due by istihsan and it becomes valid, and he is not liable upon destruction. If it is valid with generality, the first rider or wearer is fixed because he was intended from the start. In al-Khulasah: If a group of pedestrians hire camels on condition that the carrier carries whoever among them becomes ill or tired, this is invalid. His saying: (And if he restricts to a rider or wearer and then violates, he is liable) means if it perishes, because people differ in expertise in riding and wearing, and no rent is due because with liability it is inconsistent. Likewise, no rent is due if it survives, because by surviving it becomes clear that he did not violate and that the action does not weaken the animal/house? As in Ghayat al-Bayan. It is understood from his words that when he restricts, he may not sub-hire or lend, just as when he gives generality he may, and he may not deposit in the first case even by necessity, unlike the second. This is mentioned in Fusul al-Umadi in the case of the donkey that became tired on the road and he sent it to its owner with another person. His saying: (The same applies to what varies with the user regarding liability if it perishes with violation and restriction) as we mentioned. His saying: (And for what does not vary, its restriction is void, as if he stipulates one dweller, he may let others dwell) because the restriction is not beneficial due to no difference. And what harms the building, like blacksmithing and washing, is excluded as we mentioned; he does not possess it except by explicit specification. His saying: (And if he specifies type and quantity, like a load of ...)
Source pages p. 522

Commentary
This passage from Al-Bahr al-Raiq explains the conditions for valid ijarah of animals and garments, drawn from multiple sources. It begins by affirming that hiring an animal for riding or carrying, and a garment for wearing, is valid because the benefit is well-known. However, if the purpose is not a customary, beneficial use—like hiring an animal just to parade it, or a garment to decorate a shop—the ijarah is invalid because the benefit is not genuinely intended from the object. The text then provides several illustrative examples: hiring a vessel for display, a house without residing, a slave without using, even dirhams to place. Also invalid is hiring a stud male for breeding because it is not a permissible benefit. The discussion then moves to what happens after the lease: for a garment, mere ability to wear suffices for rent to accrue; but for an animal, actual use (or at least commencement of the journey) is necessary, otherwise no rent is due and liability may arise if the animal perishes while detained. A key point: if the hirer specifies the user (rider/wearer), he must adhere; violation may cause liability if the animal is destroyed. Conversely, if the contract gives absolute freedom ('ride whomever you wish'), the hirer may let anyone use it. The text details a dispute between Abu Bakr al-Razi and Abu al-Layth: if one hires an animal for carrying but rides it in return trips, is he liable? The consensus is that he may ride, but al-Razi restricts it to not combining both uses simultaneously, while al-Layth permits it generally due to custom (istihsan). The relied-upon position is that there is no liability because custom implies permission. Finally, the passage discusses the effect of restriction: if the hire is for a specific person and the hirer lets a different person use it and the animal perishes, he is liable (if the animal is of a type that varies with the user). If the use does not vary (e.g., dwelling), the restriction is void. Also, harmful uses like blacksmithing are not allowed unless explicitly specified. The last line cuts off mid-sentence on the topic of specifying type and quantity.

Key Terms
الاستحسان (al-istihsan): A method of juristic preference that overrides strict analogy in favor of custom, necessity, or ease.
التقييد (al-taqyid): The condition or restriction placed in the contract specifying the user or use.
الإطلاق (al-itlaq): In this context, giving absolute freedom to use any rider/wearer, indicated by a general expression.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the relied-upon (mufta bihi) view in the Hanafi school, if a man hires a donkey to carry wheat but rides it when returning empty and it dies, what is the ruling?
A) He must compensate the owner because he violated the contract.
B) He is not liable because custom permits riding in such situations.
C) He is liable only if the donkey was explicitly not to be ridden.
D) The owner must compensate the hirer for loss of the donkey.

Key Takeaway
The validity of ijarah depends on a customary known benefit; specifications on user are binding only if the use varies, and custom can override strict contract terms through istihsan.

Ijarah — Hire and Lease
intermediate

General Permission and Specification in Ijarah: The Riding Dispute
Commentary on ruling of general vs specific designation in ijarah, with intra-Hanafi dispute on riding when hired for carrying.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between general permission (itlaq) and specification in ijarah for riding/clothing, and state the intra-Hanafi dispute between Abu Bakr al-Razi and Abu al-Layth on liability when a hirer rides an animal hired for carrying.

Core Idea
General permission allows any rider; violation of specification incurs liability, but custom may override it.

This lesson explores a key distinction in Hanafi ijarah: when you hire an animal for riding or clothing, the contract's validity hinges on whether you specify the rider/wearer or give general permission. It also delves into an intra-Hanafi dispute about what happens when a person hired to carry goods rides the animal on the return trip—is he liable for damage? Two Hanafi authorities, Abu Bakr al-Razi and Abu al-Layth, differ on the answer, highlighting the role of custom (adah) and istihsan in Hanafi law.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two scenarios: general permission vs. specification
The matn states: if the hirer says 'ride whomever you wish' (itlaq), the contract is valid. If he specifies a particular rider and violates that, he is liable. Recognize that 'itlaq' here means generality of persons, not the usuli absolute.
2
Distinguish between hiring for carrying vs. riding
According to al-Khulasa, if hired for carrying, riding is permissible (since riding is a type of carrying). But if hired for riding, carrying is not allowed. Understand the linguistic custom: riding can include carrying, but not vice versa.
3
Present the intra-Hanafi dispute (Razi vs. Abu al-Layth)
When a person hires an animal to carry wheat but rides it on return trips, Abu Bakr al-Razi holds him liable as a usurper (ghasib) because he used the animal outside the contract. Abu al-Layth, by istihsan, says no liability because custom permits it. Note their agreement on basic permissibility but disagreement on combining both uses.
4
Analyze the basis: qiyas vs. istihsan
Razi relies on qiyas: the animal is used beyond the contract, so it's like usurpation. Abu al-Layth uses istihsan, overriding qiyas due to widespread custom. This is a classic example of Hanafi methodology.
5
Apply the ruling on 'itlaq' in practice
If the hirer gives general permission (e.g., 'ride whomever you wish'), the contract is valid and the first rider is determined. If the hirer does not specify any rider, the contract is invalid due to jahalah. But if riding occurs, it converts to valid by istihsan with no liability on destruction.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب الإجارة باب ما يجوز من الإجارة وما يكون خلافاً فيها وإن أطلق أركب وألبس من شاء وإن قيد براكب أو لابس فخالف ضمن ومثله ما يختلف وفيما لا يختلف به بطل تقييده كما لو شرط سكنى واحد له أن يسكن غيره فإن هلك اهـ. وفي الخلاصة : لو حبس الدابة ليلة حتى أصبح فردها ولم يركب عليها لا أجر عليه اهـ. وفيها أيضاً : رجل استأجر دابة ليحمل عليها له أن يركبها، وإن استأجرها ليركبها ليس له أن يحمل عليها ولو حمل عليها فلا أجر عليه لأن الركوب يسمى حملاً يقال ركب فلان وحمل معه غيره ولا يسمى الحمل ركوباً أصلاً .اهـ. وفي فصول العمادي معزياً إلى الذخيرة : استأجر دابة ليحمل عليها حنطة من موضع إلى منزله يوماً إلى الليل وكان يحمل الحنطة إلى منزله وكلما رجع كان يركبها فعطبت الدابة قال أبو بكر الرازي: يضمن لأنه استأجرها للحمل دون الركوب فكان غاصباً للركوب وقال الفقيه أبو الليث : في الاستحسان لا يضمن لأن العادة جرت فيما بين الناس بذلك فصار مأذوناً فيه دلالة وإن لم يأذن بالإفصاح اهـ. فالحاصل أنهم اتفقوا على أن من استأجرها للحمل له أن يركبها لكن الرازي قيده بأن لا يجمع بينهما والفقيه عممه . قوله : (وإن أطلق اركب والبس من شاء أراد بالإطلاق التعميم بأن يأتي بلفظ دال على العموم من غير تقييد براكب ولابس معين لا الإطلاق المصطلح عليه عند الأصوليين، فلو قال على أن تركب من شئت أو تلبس من شئت صح العقد، ولو استأجرها للركوب ولم يسم من يركبه لا تصح الإجارة والفرق أن في الثانية صار الركوبان مثلاً من شخصين كالجنسين فيكون المعقود عليه مجهولاً فلا يصح، وفي الأولى رضي المالك بالقدر الذي يحصل في ضمن الركوب فصار المعقود عليه معلوماً، وإذا فسدت فلو أركبها أو أركب بنفسه وجب المسمى استحساناً وتنقلب صحيحة ولا ضمان عليه عند الهلاك، وإذا صحت عند التعميم تعين أول راكب أو لابس لتعينه مراداً من الأصل فصار كالنص عليه ابتداء. وفي الخلاصة: وإذا تكارى قوم مشاة إبلاً على أن المكاري يحمل من مرض منهم أو من عي منهم فهذا فاسد
Book of Ijarah, Chapter on what is permissible in Ijarah and what constitutes a violation therein. And if he [the hirer] gives general permission (itlaq) to ride and clothe whomever he wishes, but if he specifies a rider or wearer and then violates [the specification], he is liable (daman). And similar [in ruling] is what differs [with the user], and in what does not differ, its specification is void, as if he stipulated a single occupant, he has the right to house someone else. Then if it is destroyed, end quote. And in al-Khulasa: If he detained the animal for a night until morning, then returned it without riding it, no hire is due. And in it also: A man hires an animal to carry [something], he may ride it. But if he hires it to ride, he may not carry [anything] on it. And if he carries [something], no hire is due because riding is called carrying; it is said 'so-and-so rode and carried with him someone else', but carrying is not called riding at all. End quote. And in Fusul al-Imadi, citing al-Dhakhira: He hired an animal to carry wheat from a place to his house for a day until night, and he was carrying wheat to his house, and whenever he returned he would ride it, and the animal was ruined. Abu Bakr al-Razi said: He is liable because he hired it for carrying, not riding, so he was a usurper (ghasib) of the riding. And Imam Abu al-Layth said: By istihsan, he is not liable because custom (adah) has become established among people to that effect, so he is implicitly permitted, even if not explicitly authorized. End quote. The upshot is that they agreed that one who hires an animal for carrying may ride it, but al-Razi conditioned that he not combine [both], while Abu al-Layth generalized [it]. Then his saying: (And if he gives general permission, ride and clothe whomever he wishes) — he intended by 'general permission' general authorization, by using a word indicating generality without specifying a particular rider or wearer, not the technical 'absolute' (itlaq) in the terminology of usuliyyun. So if he says, 'on condition that you ride whomever you wish or clothe whomever you wish', the contract is valid. And if he hired it for riding but did not specify the rider, the ijarah is not valid. The difference is that in the second case, riding by e.g. two persons becomes like two different types, so the subject matter is unknown and thus invalid. In the first case, the owner consented to the quantity that occurs within the riding, so the subject matter becomes known. And if it is invalid, then if he rode it or rode himself, the named hire is due by istihsan and it converts to a valid contract, and there is no liability upon destruction. And if it is valid by general authorization, the first rider or wearer is determined, because he is intended from the beginning, so it is as if he was specified initially. And in al-Khulasa: If a group of pedestrians hire camels on condition that the carrier (mukari) carries whoever among them becomes sick or exhausted, this is invalid.
Source pages p. 523

Commentary
This passage opens with the ruling on general versus specific permission in ijarah when hiring an animal for riding or clothing. The matn states: if the hirer gives general permission (itlaq), he may choose any rider or wearer; if he specifies a particular person and then violates that specification, he is liable. The same applies to any use that differs with the user—if the use varies significantly, violation incurs liability; if it does not differ, the specification is void. The author then cites supporting rulings from al-Khulasa and Fusul al-Imadi. The first citation clarifies that merely detaining an animal without use does not require payment. The second citation distinguishes between hiring for carrying (allowed to ride) and hiring for riding (not allowed to carry), based on linguistic custom: riding is a form of carrying, but not vice versa. The third citation presents an intra-Hanafi dispute between Abu Bakr al-Razi and Abu al-Layth about a person who hires an animal to carry wheat but rides it on return trips. Al-Razi holds him liable as a usurper, while Abu al-Layth uses istihsan to absolve him due to customary practice. The author notes they agree on permissibility of riding when hired for carrying, but differ on combining both simultaneously. The commentary then explains the meaning of 'itlaq' in this context: it means general authorization, not the technical absolute of usuliyyun. The distinction is crucial: if the hirer says 'ride whomever you wish', the contract is valid because the owner consented to the general quantity of riding; but if he hires for riding without naming the rider, the contract is invalid due to uncertainty (jahalah). If the contract is invalid, riding still obligates the named hire by istihsan, converting it to a valid contract with no liability for destruction. When the contract is valid by general authorization, the first rider is automatically determined.

Key Terms
الإطلاق (al-itlaq): In this context, it means general authorization by using a word of generality (e.g., 'whomever you wish'), not the technical usuli meaning of absence of qayd.
الاستحسان (al-istihsan): A juristic preference method used here to override strict analogy (qiyas) based on custom (adah).
الغاصب (al-ghasib): One who unlawfully takes and uses property of another; here, the hirer who rides without permission is considered a usurper.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the text, what is the difference between Abu Bakr al-Razi's position and Abu al-Layth's position on a person who hires an animal to carry wheat but rides it on the return trip?
A) Razi says no liability; Abu al-Layth says liability
B) Razi says liability if the rider combines both riding and carrying simultaneously; Abu al-Layth says no liability unconditionally
C) Both agree on full liability
D) Both agree on no liability

Key Takeaway
Intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf on liability for riding when hired for carrying illustrates the tension between strict qiyas and istihsan based on custom.

Liability for Violating Specification of Rider or Wearer in Ijarah
Commentary on the matn ruling: if a rider is specified and violated, the hirer is liable.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling when a hirer specifies a rider or wearer and violates the condition, and explain the consequence for the hire payment.

Core Idea
If the hirer violates a specification and the animal is ruined, he is liable and no hire is due.

In ijarah (hire) contracts, the hirer may specify a particular rider or wearer. If he violates this condition by using another person, what is the legal effect? This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq explains that if the animal is ruined, the hirer is liable and no hire is due. Even if the animal survives, no hire is due because the survival shows there was no actual violation. This ruling is based on the fact that people differ in their riding skills and weight, making the specification meaningful.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and the condition
What is the act? The hirer specifies a particular rider or wearer. Then he violates by using a different person. Identify the contract: ijarah of a riding animal or clothing.
The specification is part of the contract terms.
2
State the ruling when the animal is ruined
If the animal is ruined, the hirer is liable (daman). Additionally, no hire (ujrah) is due because the hirer is under liability (daman), which is incompatible with receiving hire.
Liability here means compensation for the value of the animal.
3
State the ruling when the animal survives
If the animal survives, also no hire is due. The reason: the survival indicates that the violation did not actually cause any difference, so it becomes clear that there was no true violation of the specification. Thus, the hirer's use was not unauthorized.
The contract is not entirely void; the hire is waived because the condition was not materially breached.
4
Explain the rationale
Why does specification matter? Because people differ in knowledge of riding and clothing (i.e., skill, weight, impact). The specification is meaningful; violation can cause damage. Therefore, the liability is justified.
If there were no difference, the specification would be void, but here it is valid.
5
Note the distinction from other uses
If the use does not differ (like single occupancy), specification is void. But for riders, it does differ.
This will be covered in the next lesson.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (وإن قيد براكب أو لابس فخالف ضمن يعني إذا عطبت لأن الناس يتفاوتون في العلم بالركوب واللبس ولا أجر عليه لأنه مع الضمان ممتنع ، وكذا لا أجر عليه إن سلم لأنه لما سلم تبين أنه لم يخالف وأنه مما لا يوهن الدار ؛ كذا في غاية البيان. واستفيد من كلامه أنه إذا قيد ليس له الإجارة والإعارة كما أنه إذا عمم له ذلك وليس له الإيداع في الأول ولو لضرورة دون الثاني ؛ ذكره في فصول العمادي في مسألة ما إذا عي الحمار في الطريق فأرسله إلى صاحبه مع آخر قوله : (ومثله ما يختلف باختلاف المستعمل في كونه يضمن إذا عطبت مع المخالفة والتقييد لما قدمناه قوله : (وفيما لا يختلف به بطل تقييده به كما لو شرط سکنی واحد له أن يسكن غيره لأن التقييد غير مفيد لعدم التفاوت والذي يضر بالبناء كالحدادة والقصارة خارج على ما قدمناه فلا يملكه إلا بالتنصيص
His saying: (And if he specifies a rider or wearer and then violates, he is liable) — meaning if it is ruined, because people differ in knowledge of riding and clothing, and no hire is due because with liability it is prevented. Similarly, no hire is due if it survives, because since it survived, it became clear that he did not violate and that it is something that does not weaken the structure. As in Ghayat al-Bayan. And it is understood from his words that when he specifies, he does not have the right to sub-lease (ijarah) or lend (i'arrah), just as when he generalizes he has that. And he does not have the right to deposit (wadi'ah) in the first case, even for necessity, unlike the second. Mentioned in Fusul al-Imadi in the issue of when a donkey tires on the road and he sends it to its owner with someone else. His saying: (And similar is what differs with the user) — meaning that he is liable if it is ruined with violation and specification, as we have explained. His saying: (And in what does not differ, its specification becomes void) — as if he stipulates single occupancy, he has the right to house someone else, because the specification is not beneficial due to lack of difference. And that which harms the building, like blacksmithing or fulling, is excluded as we have stated, so he does not own [the right to do it] except by explicit stipulation.
Source pages p. 523

Commentary
This passage continues the commentary on the matn rulings about specification violation. The sharh explains that if the hirer specifies a particular rider or wearer and uses another person, and the animal is ruined, he is liable because people differ in skill and weight; thus the violation is material. No hire is due during liability. If the animal survives, no hire is due because it becomes clear there was no actual violation. The commentator derives that specification prevents sub-leasing or lending, whereas general permission allows them; but deposit is never allowed even with general permission, unless necessity. The explication then treats the matn statement 'and similar is what differs': if the user varies in a way that affects the property (like different riding skills), violation with specification triggers liability. For those that do not differ (like single occupancy vs another person), the specification is void because the stipulated condition is meaningless. Examples of harmful uses like blacksmithing (heavy pounding) are excluded; they require explicit permission.

Key Terms
التقييد (al-taqyid): The act of specifying a particular rider, wearer, or use in the ijarah contract.
المخالفة (al-mukhalafah): Breach of the specified condition by using a different person or use.
الضمان (al-daman): Liability for compensation when the animal is ruined due to violation.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the ruling on hire if the hirer specifies a rider, uses another, and the animal survives?
A) Full hire is due because the contract was valid
B) No hire is due because it becomes clear no violation occurred
C) Hire is doubled as penalty
D) Hire is halved because of the violation

Key Takeaway
In specification violation, if the animal is ruined, the hirer is liable and no hire is due; if it survives, also no hire due because survival negates the violation.

Implications of Specification: Sub-lease, Lending, and Distinction of Uses
Further commentary on derivations from the initial ruling.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between uses that differ (rider skill) and those that do not (single occupancy), and state the rules for sub-leasing, lending, and deposit when specification is present.

Core Idea
When a specification is given, the hirer cannot sub-lease or lend; when no specification, he can, but deposit is never allowed without necessity.

The previous lesson covered the core liability ruling. This lesson explores the further derivations: if the hirer specifies a rider, he loses the right to sub-lease (ijarah) or lend (i'arah) the animal, whereas if he did not specify (general hire), he retains those rights. However, he never has the right to deposit (wadi'ah) the animal with another, even for necessity, unless in the general case? Actually, the text says 'in the first case, even for necessity, unlike the second.' This lesson also distinguishes between uses that differ (like riding skill) which trigger liability upon violation, and uses that do not differ (like single occupancy) which make the specification void. Additionally, harmful uses like blacksmithing require explicit permission.

Guided Path
1
Identify the derived rules from specification
From the commentator's words, understand that if the hirer specifies a rider, he cannot sub-lease or lend the animal. If he did not specify (general), he can. But he cannot deposit in either case, except in the general case? Actually, the text says deposit is not allowed in the first (specified) even for necessity, but in the second (general) it is allowed. So that's the rule.
The reference to Fusul al-Imadi about the donkey tiring on the road is an application.
2
Understand the distinction between uses that differ and those that do not
The matn says: 'and similar is what differs with the user' — meaning if the user's identity affects the property (like rider skill/weight), then specification is meaningful and violation triggers liability. For uses that do not differ (like single occupancy vs another person), the specification is void because the condition is not beneficial. The hirer can then use anyone.
3
Identify excluded harmful uses
Uses that harm the building like blacksmithing (heavy pounding) or fulling (stamping) are outside the default permission; they require explicit stipulation. So even under a general hire, the hirer cannot engage in such activities unless specified.
These are 'adhuru' — harmful.
4
Summarize the practical implications
When hiring with specification, the hirer's rights are restricted: no sub-lease, no lending, no deposit. Without specification, he has more flexibility but still cannot do harmful acts without permission.

Source Text
